<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg"><img alt="STS-132 Official Crew Photo.jpg" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg/220px-STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg/330px-STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg/440px-STS-132_Official_Crew_Photo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><br/>

<div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a></div>
<div style="width:100%">
<div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">← <a href="/wiki/STS-131" title="STS-131">STS-131</a></div>
<div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/STS-133" title="STS-133">STS-133</a> →</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>STS-132</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISS_assembly_sequence" title="ISS assembly sequence">ISS assembly</a> flight <b>ULF4</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifest_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-manifest-5">[5]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> mission, during which Space Shuttle <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i> docked with the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> on 16 May 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-docktweet_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-docktweet-6">[6]</a></sup> STS-132 was launched from the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> on 14 May 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sched_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-sched-3">[3]</a></sup> The primary payload was the Russian <a href="/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)" title="Rassvet (ISS module)"><i>Rassvet</i> Mini-Research Module</a>, along with an Integrated Cargo Carrier-Vertical Light Deployable (ICC-VLD). <i>Atlantis</i> landed at the Kennedy Space Center on 26 May 2010.</p>
<p>STS-132 was initially scheduled to be the final flight of <i>Atlantis</i>, provided that the <a href="/wiki/STS-3xx" title="STS-3xx">STS-335</a>/<a href="/wiki/STS-135" title="STS-135">STS-135</a> Launch On Need rescue mission would not be needed. However, in February 2011, NASA declared that the final mission of <i>Atlantis</i> and of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a>, <a href="/wiki/STS-135" title="STS-135">STS-135</a>, would be flown regardless of the funding situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h3><span id="Mini-Research_Module_1_(MRM_1)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mini-Research_Module_1_.28MRM_1.29">Mini-Research Module 1 (MRM 1)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mini-Research Module 1 (MRM 1)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)" title="Rassvet (ISS module)">Rassvet (ISS module)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MRM1_Rassvet.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="1022" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/MRM1_Rassvet.png/275px-MRM1_Rassvet.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/MRM1_Rassvet.png/413px-MRM1_Rassvet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/MRM1_Rassvet.png/550px-MRM1_Rassvet.png 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MRM1_Rassvet.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
MRM-1 Rassvet docking module</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>STS-132 carried the Russian <a href="/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)" title="Rassvet (ISS module)">Rassvet Mini-Research Module 1</a> to the International Space Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <i>Rassvet</i> means "dawn" in Russian. The module was built by Russian aerospace company <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S.P._Korolev_Rocket_and_Space_Corporation_Energia" title="S.P. Korolev Rocket and Space Corporation Energia">Energia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Rassvet arrived at the Kennedy Space Center (KSC) aboard an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonov_124" title="Antonov 124">Antonov 124</a> cargo plane on 17 December 2009 at about 13:00 <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">EST</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> After it was unloaded from the Antonov, the module was transported to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrotech" title="Astrotech">Astrotech</a> processing bay in Cape Canaveral to undergo preparations for launch.</p>
<p>An airlock and radiation heat exchanger to be used for outfitting the Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module)" title="Nauka (ISS module)">Nauka</a></i> Module (to be launched in 2012), a spare elbow part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Robotic_Arm" title="European Robotic Arm">European Robotic Arm</a> (ERA) and a portable work platform for science hardware for performing experiments in outer space were externally mounted on Rassvet in its launch configuration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Payload_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Payload-14">[14]</a></sup> Russian and US cargo to be delivered will also be accommodated inside the module. The volume for cargo and science inside MRM1 is 5 cubic meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Rassvet was outfitted with ISS standard grapple fixtures that allowed the module to be unloaded from the payload bay of <i>Atlantis</i> using the station's robotic arm.</p>
<h3><span id="Integrated_Cargo_Carrier-Vertical_Light_Deployable_(ICC-VLD2)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated_Cargo_Carrier-Vertical_Light_Deployable_.28ICC-VLD2.29">Integrated Cargo Carrier-Vertical Light Deployable (ICC-VLD2)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Integrated Cargo Carrier-Vertical Light Deployable (ICC-VLD2)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="775" data-file-width="961" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG/275px-STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG/413px-STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG/550px-STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS132_ICC-VLD_Lauch_Return_Configuration.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ICC-VLD2 launch and return configurations</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Also on board <i>Atlantis</i> was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrated_Cargo_Carrier" title="Integrated Cargo Carrier">Integrated Cargo Carrier</a>-Vertical Light Deployable (ICC-VLD2) pallet, holding a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ku-band" title="Ku-band">K<sub>u</sub>-band</a> Space to Ground Antenna (SGANT), the SGANT boom assembly, an Enhanced Orbital replacement Unit (ORU) Temporary Platform (EOTP) for the Canadian <a href="/wiki/Dextre" title="Dextre">Dextre</a> robotic arm extension, Video and Power Grapple fixtures (PVGF) and six new battery ORUs. The six new batteries replaced older ones on the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure#P6.2C_S6_trusses" title="Integrated Truss Structure">P6 truss</a> of the ISS. The old batteries were placed on the ICC-VLD pallet for return to Earth. The EOTP was built by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MacDonald_Dettwiler" title="MacDonald Dettwiler">MacDonald, Dettwiler and Associates</a> Ltd. (MDA) of Brampton, Ontario, Canada, for <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ICC pallet is constructed of aluminum. It is approximately 8 feet (2.4 m) long, 13 feet (4.0 m) wide and 10 inches thick. The empty weight of the pallet is 2,645 pounds. The total weight of ICC–VLD and the ORUs is approximately 8,330 pounds. ICC-VLD return mass is 2,933 kilograms (6,466 lb).</p>
<p>The ICC-VLD was berthed in the center of the shuttle's payload bay for both launch and reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_items">Other items</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other items">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to the standard Official Flight Kit (OFK) flown inside a locker on the mid-deck, two Light Weight Tool Stowage Assemblies were modified to fly memorabilia and then were stowed to the left and right of <i>Atlantis</i>' airlock in the shuttle's payload bay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>A compact disk (CD) containing the digital copies of all entries submitted to NASA's Space Shuttle Program Commemorative Patch Contest<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> was also flown aboard <i>Atlantis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The contest was organized by the Space Shuttle Program to mark the end of the shuttle era. The winning patch was designed by Mr. Blake Dumesnil of <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Sundstrand" title="Hamilton Sundstrand">Hamilton Sundstrand</a>, Johnson Space Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A panel of NASA judges, including shuttle program manager John Shannon, Leroy Cain, and three other shuttle program managers including former astronaut <a href="/wiki/John_Casper" title="John Casper">John Casper</a>, selected the winning patch from a pool of 85 entries by NASA employees and contractors.</p>
<p>Seventeen handcrafted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beads" title="Beads">beads</a> made by nine different artists across North America were also on board <i>Atlantis</i> during the STS-132 mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> NASA teamed up with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Beads_of_Courage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Beads of Courage (page does not exist)">Beads of Courage</a>, Inc., an approved public charity, to bring hope and inspiration to children coping with serious illnesses through the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Beads_in_Space&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Beads in Space (page does not exist)">Beads in Space</a> project. The Beads in Space project is the brainchild of Jamie Newton, an employee at the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in Huntsville, Alabama. The 17 beads weigh eight ounces and were selected after a contest organized by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Beads_of_Courage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Beads of Courage (page does not exist)">Beads of Courage</a> that attracted 54 beads.</p>
<p>Also on board <i>Atlantis</i> was a 4-inch long wood sample of Sir <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_tree" title="Apple tree">apple tree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The piece from the original tree that supposedly inspired Newton's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_gravity" title="Theory of gravity">theory of gravity</a>, along with a picture of Newton, were taken into orbit by astronaut Piers Sellers. The wood is part of the collection of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> archives in London, and was returned there following the flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, a flag from <a href="/wiki/Clarkson_University" title="Clarkson University">Clarkson University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_(village),_New_York" title="Potsdam (village), New York">Potsdam, New York</a>, flew on board shuttle <i>Atlantis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> It was there in honor of STS-132 lead shuttle flight director, Michael L. Sarafin, who is an alumnus of the Clarkson University.</p>
<p><i>A comprehensive list of STS-132 items that were carried aboard Atlantis and their descriptions can be found in the Official Flight Kit</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>

</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_background_and_milestones">Mission background and milestones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mission background and milestones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sts132_mission_poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3160" data-file-width="4200" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Sts132_mission_poster.jpg/200px-Sts132_mission_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Sts132_mission_poster.jpg/300px-Sts132_mission_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Sts132_mission_poster.jpg/400px-Sts132_mission_poster.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sts132_mission_poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mission poster</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="1724" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg/220px-4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg/330px-4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg/440px-4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:4th_FW_Strike_Eagles_assist_shuttle_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Atlantis heads into space while a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-15E_Strike_Eagle" title="F-15E Strike Eagle">F-15E Strike Eagle</a> jets patrolled the skies over Kennedy Space Center.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The mission marked:</p>
<ul>
<li>163rd NASA manned space flight</li>
<li>132nd shuttle mission since <a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a></li>
<li>32nd flight of <i>Atlantis</i></li>
<li>34th shuttle mission to the ISS</li>
<li>11th flight of <i>Atlantis</i> to the ISS</li>
<li>3rd shuttle flight in 2010</li>
<li>107th post-<i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Challenger</a></i> mission</li>
<li>19th post-<i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">Columbia</a></i> mission</li>
</ul>
<p>NASA arranged a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Tweetup" title="NASA Tweetup">Tweetup</a> to cover the launch of the STS-132 mission. 150 people attended the event from more than 30 US states, the District of Columbia, Puerto Rico, Belgium, the Netherlands, New Zealand and the United Kingdom. The Tweetup participants met with shuttle technicians, managers, engineers and astronauts, took a tour of the Kennedy Space Center and viewed the launch of <i>Atlantis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_experiments">Mission experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mission experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Atlantis crew worked with several short-term experiments during their mission. The shuttle transported new <a href="/wiki/Long-term_experiment" title="Long-term experiment">long-term experiments</a> to the ISS. At the end of the mission, <i>Atlantis</i> returned some of the completed experiments from the ISS.</p>
<p>Short-term experiments included:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Micro-2</i>: Researchers from <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute">Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute</a> sent microorganisms to investigate new ways of preventing the formation and spread of clusters of bacteria (biofilms), that could pose a threat to the health of astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> After the shuttle landed, the resulting biofilms were examined to see how their growth and development were impacted by microgravity.</li>
<li><i>Hypersole</i>: Hypersole is a Canadian research project that plans to investigate sudden changes in skin sensitivity experienced by some astronauts in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The researchers hope to understand more about how the skin sensitivity of the soles of the feet affect the human balance. Three STS-132 crew members participated in identical trials before the launch and immediately upon landing. The trials were also repeated on five astronauts scheduled to fly on the <a href="/wiki/STS-133" title="STS-133">STS-133</a> and <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a> missions. Project findings are expected to add significant knowledge to existing studies of aging and to be beneficial for the elderly and people who suffer from balance problems.</li>
<li><i>Shuttle Ionospheric Modification with Pulsed Localized Exhaust Experiments (SIMPLEX)</i> – STS-132 crew performed the SIMPLEX burn on Flight Day 12. The experiment investigates plasma turbulence driven by shuttle exhaust in the ionosphere using ground-based radars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIMPLEX_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIMPLEX-31">[31]</a></sup> The processes by which chemical releases can produce plasma turbulence are quantified with the SIMPLEX measurements. Plasma turbulence can affect military navigation and communications using radio systems. They can also be used to promote communications by opening radio channels at abnormally high frequencies.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shuttle_processing">Shuttle processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Shuttle processing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The mission's <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">external tank</a>, ET-136, began its 900-mile (1,400 km), six-day journey across the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> from NASA's <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, Louisiana, on 24 February 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> ET-136 measured 154 feet (47 m) long and 28 feet (8.5 m) in diameter. The solid rocket booster retrieval ship <i><a href="/wiki/MV_Liberty_Star" title="MV Liberty Star">Liberty Star</a></i> towed the ET in the enclosed barge <i>Pegasus</i>. After docking in the turn basin at the Kennedy Space Center, the tank was offloaded and driven to the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">Vehicle Assembly Building</a> (VAB) on 1 March 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 29 March 2010, workers attached ET-136 to its <a href="/wiki/Solid_rocket_booster" title="Solid rocket booster">solid rocket boosters</a>. A crane lifted the ET into high bay No. 1 inside the VAB. The day-long process was completed around 18:00 EDT, as the tank was bolted to <i>Atlantis</i>'s twin solid rocket boosters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Atlantis</i> rolled out of its processing bay (OPF-1) around 07:00 EDT on 13 April 2010. The shuttle entered the VAB around 11:00 EDT for attachment to its external tank and solid rocket boosters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Given that this was at the time believed to be <i>Atlantis</i>' final rollover for a mission, the shuttle stopped for several hours en route to the VAB, allowing engineers and technicians to pose for photographs with the orbiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The rollover occurred exactly 25 years after <i>Atlantis</i> first arrived at the Kennedy Space Center, after a cross-country trip from the shuttle factory in <a href="/wiki/Palmdale,_California" title="Palmdale, California">Palmdale, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The path to the rollover was without any incidents of major concern, with only 22 Interim Problem Reports (IPRs) noted during <i>Atlantis'</i> flow since its return from the <a href="/wiki/STS-129" title="STS-129">STS-129</a> mission in November 2009.</p>
<p>The transport canister containing the STS-132 payload arrived at Pad 39A on 15 April 2010 ahead of <i>Atlantis'</i> rollover to the launch pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The canister was shaped like the shuttle's 60-foot (18 m)-long payload bay. Packed inside it were the MRM-1 module and the cargo-carrying pallet ICC-VLD.</p>
<p>Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> began its rollout to launch pad 39A at 23:31 EDT on 21 April 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The complete shuttle stack and mobile launch platform were secured to the launch pad's structure at 6:03 EDT on 22 April 2010. The 3.4 mi (<span class="nowrap">5.5 kilometres (3.4 mi)</span>) trek took 6 hours and 32 minutes to complete. The rollout was originally planned for the evening of 19 April 2010, but wet weather and thunderstorms on the Space Coast caused several delays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>STS-132's payload was installed in the shuttle's cargo bay on 25 April 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pad engineers preparing <i>Atlantis</i> had noticed paint peeling from shuttle's main payload, the MRM-1 module. Although the problem was declared to have no impact on the operation of MRM-1, it holds a potential threat of releasing debris on orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Engineers also noted MRM-1 cycled its Fire and Smoke detector self test several times. Similar events occurred during <i>Atlantis'</i> <a href="/wiki/STS-129" title="STS-129">STS-129</a> mission in November 2009 when Shuttle and Station crew were awakened consecutive nights by false depressurization and fire alarms that originated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRM-2" title="MRM-2">MRM-2</a> (Poisk) module.</p>
<p>An agency-wide Flight Readiness Review (FRR) was held at KSC in Florida on 5 May 2010 to discuss Atlantis' launch preparations. At the end of the review, top NASA managers made the decision to officially set the launch for 14 May 2010 at 14:20 EDT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> NASA held a post news conference to brief about the results of the FRR. The briefing was broadcast on NASA TV and was attended by NASA's Associate Administrator for Space Operations, William Gerstenmaier, Space Shuttle Program Manager John Shannon and Space Shuttle Launch Director, Michael Leinbach. Mr. Shannon mentioned that, firstly, ceramic inserts around <i>Atlantis</i>' windows and forward rocket pod were tested after an insert loosened during Discovery's re-entry on <a href="/wiki/STS-131" title="STS-131">STS-131</a>, posing a potential impact threat. The inserts had been re-installed on to <i>Atlantis</i> using a thicker braided cord to reduce the chances of a backing out. Secondly, it was mentioned that engineers had reviewed work to confirm that all systems on <i>Atlantis</i>' K<sub>u</sub> band antenna were in place. The testing had been provoked after the failure of that communication system during STS-131. Mr. Leinbach also acknowledged the skills and experience of the engineering teams and thanked the engineers who had successfully resolved hypergolic loading issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Hypergolics are chemicals that ignite when they come in contact with each other. The propellants are used in the reaction control system that steers the shuttle in space.</p>
<p>A booster rocket segment that had first flown 25 years before on <i>Atlantis</i>' maiden flight (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/STS-51J" title="STS-51J">STS-51-J</a>) was used to help fly STS-132.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The aft dome on the left solid rocket booster that lifted off to support <i>Atlantis</i>' STS-132 mission first launched STS-51-J on 3 October 1985. Including STS-132, 18 of <i>Atlantis</i>' 32 flights were represented by the boosters' segments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_preparations">Launch preparations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Launch preparations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Atlantis'</i> astronauts traveled from Johnson Space Center, Houston to the KSC launch site on 10 May 2010 to prepare for the launch. The crew, arriving in four <a href="/wiki/Northrop_T-38_Talon" title="Northrop T-38 Talon">Northrop T-38 Talon</a> jets, landed on the Shuttle Landing Facility around 18:49 EDT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The official countdown to liftoff started on 11 May 2010 after the countdown clocks at KSC were activated at 16:00 EDT, ticking backward from the T-43 hour mark.</p>
<p>Program managers completed the L-2 Mission Management Team (MMT) meeting on 12 May 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> At the end of the 18-minute-long meeting management team officially cleared <i>Atlantis</i> for launch. NASA held a pre-launch news conference to reveal the outcomes of the MMT and to brief the press on the upcoming launch. The news conference was attended by Chair, pre-launch mission management team, Mike Moses, Mike Leinbach and STS-132 weather officer, Todd McNamara. The weather officer spoke of a favorable launch weather forecast due to a high pressure weather pattern and despite a low cloud ceiling, calling a 70 percent chance of favorable conditions at launch time. He further elaborated on the predicted weather conditions at the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_abort_modes" title="Space Shuttle abort modes">Transoceanic Abort Landing</a> (TAL) sites: <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza_Air_Base" title="Zaragoza Air Base">Zaragoza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mor%C3%B3n_Air_Base" title="Morón Air Base">Moron</a> in Spain, and <a href="/wiki/Istres-Le_Tub%C3%A9_Air_Base" title="Istres-Le Tubé Air Base">Istres</a>, France, in case of an emergency.</p>
<p>The Space Shuttle Program MMT met at 04:15 EDT on 14 May 2010 and gave a go to begin loading <i>Atlantis'</i> ET with liquid oxygen and liquid hydrogen. The fuel tanking operation began on time at 04:55 EDT and was completed within three hours at 07:56 EDT, with replenishment fuel being added throughout the countdown.</p>
<p>Crew preparations for the launch day began at 05:00 EDT following an eight-hour overnight sleep. An hour later they completed their final medical check ups. Crew suiting began around 10:00 EDT and the astronauts departed for the launch pad at 10:30 EDT. At around 11:00 EDT, first Commander Ham ingressed the shuttle first and strapped into his seat, followed by pilot Antonelli, Mission Specialists Bowen, Sellers, Reisman and Good in order. Inside the orbiter, all six astronauts performed checks with ground controllers to verify that communications links work properly. With all astronauts on board, <i>Atlantis'</i> hatch was closed and latched for the flight. Inside the White Room, the closeout crew finished their job by pressurizing the crew cabin and checking for leaks before leaving the pad.</p>
<p>Launch day countdown procedures went without any major problems; however, <i>Atlantis</i> encountered two minor issues. The Final Inspection Team looking for ice and frost buildup on the ET had spotted a small <a href="/wiki/Stress_fracture" title="Stress fracture">stress fracture</a> on an umbilical strut. Later, during the post-launch news conference, the chair of NASA's pre-launch mission management team, Mike Moses, said that this was not unusual. Engineers also resolved any concerns about a loose <a href="/wiki/Ball_bearing" title="Ball bearing">ball bearing</a> found near the shuttle's payload bay days earlier. The bearing was determined to likely be from a camera system, and was ultimately ruled out as a concern.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_timeline">Mission timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mission timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="14_May_(Flight_Day_1_–_Launch)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="14_May_.28Flight_Day_1_.E2.80.93_Launch.29">14 May (Flight Day 1 – Launch)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 14 May (Flight Day 1 – Launch)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumb tright">

Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> launches from Kennedy Space Center, 14 May 2010.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The launch of Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> occurred on time at 18:20 UTC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> with launch commentator <a href="/wiki/George_Diller" title="George Diller">George Diller</a> saying that the shuttle was "reaching the crest of its historic achievements in space". Powered flight conformed to the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle#Launch" title="Space Shuttle">standard timeline</a>, with main engine cutoff (MECO) occurring at 8 minutes and 32 seconds Mission Elapsed Time (MET). The External Tank, ET-136, separated from the shuttle 15 seconds later at 8:47 MET.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> A further boost from the Orbital Maneuvering System (OMS) engines was not required due to the nominal MECO, and <i>Atlantis</i> settled into its planned preliminary orbit. A subsequent NC-1 engine firing of about 26 seconds adjusted the orbital path of the shuttle to match that of the International Space Station (ISS), by altering the shuttle's velocity by about 41 ft/s (12 m/s).</p>
<p>NASA held a post-launch news conference with Bill Gerstenmaier, Alexey Krasnov (chief of the Piloted Programs Directorate at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency" title="Russian Federal Space Agency">Russian Federal Space Agency</a>), Mike Moses and Mike Leinbach. During the conference, Gerstenmaier made mention of a piece of space junk that could potentially have impacted <i>Atlantis</i>' planned arrival at the ISS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>More than 39,000 guests, including television host <a href="/wiki/David_Letterman" title="David Letterman">David Letterman</a>, ex-<a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo</a> astronaut <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a>, and former NASA administrator <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin" title="Michael D. Griffin">Michael Griffin</a>, witnessed the launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The Russian deputy prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Ivanov" title="Sergei Ivanov">Sergei Ivanov</a>, and the head of the Russian Space Federal Agency, <a href="/wiki/Anatoly_Perminov" title="Anatoly Perminov">Anatoly Perminov</a>, were also present at KSC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once in orbit, the crew opened the shuttle's payload bay doors, activated the radiators and deployed the K<sub>u</sub> band antenna successfully. They also completed a checkout of the orbiter's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuttle_Remote_Manipulator_System" title="Shuttle Remote Manipulator System">Shuttle Remote Manipulator System</a> (SRMS). The crew was also successful in downlinking all imagery from Atlantis' umbilical well cameras, along with crew video of ET-136, for review by imagery experts in the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Preliminary inspections showed that ET-136 was very clean and had performed well during the ascent, with only a few foam liberation incidents visible.</p>
<p>It is worth noting that the launch of Atlantis on STS-132 was supposed to be its last. The mission patch depicted Atlantis flying into the sunset as she retired, and the Space Shuttle Program patches on sale at the Kennedy Space Center for the launch showed the years 1981–2010 for the program. The programme guide magazine to the launch also cited the launch as the final flight of Space Shuttle Atlantis. However, on its return Atlantis was readied as a rescue shuttle for the (at that time) two remaining missions of STS-133 and STS-134. It could be launched on need if there was a problem and if not, it would be stood down. Then, later in 2010, NASA decided to fly the previously provision STS-135 mission and chose Atlantis for the flight given that Discover and Endeavour were earmarked for STS-133 and STS-134 respectively. The funding was found and a decision was taken to fly STS-135 as the final Space Shuttle mission, with Atlantis as the orbiter. The STS-135 mission launched in July 2011, just over a year after Atlantis's launch on the STS-132 mission.</p>
<h3><span id="15_May_(Flight_Day_2_–_TPS_survey)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="15_May_.28Flight_Day_2_.E2.80.93_TPS_survey.29">15 May (Flight Day 2 – TPS survey)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 15 May (Flight Day 2 – TPS survey)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg/220px-Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg/330px-Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg/440px-Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cargo_bay_of_Atlantis_on_STS-132.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Atlantis' cargo bay and its vertical stabilizer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The crew members aboard <i>Atlantis</i> began their first full day in space at 08:20 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The day was primarily devoted to inspecting <i>Atlantis's</i> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_thermal_protection_system" title="Space Shuttle thermal protection system">thermal protection system</a>, using the shuttle's robotic arm and the <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Boom_Sensor_System" title="Orbiter Boom Sensor System">Orbiter Boom Sensor System</a> (OBSS) to look for any signs of launch damage. Before the thermal protection checkout began, the crew encountered a problem with the Laser Dynamic Range Imager (LDRI) and the Intensified TV Camera (ITVC), due to a snagged cable in that system's pan and tilt unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> As a result, Mission Control decided to switch to the less-capable backup sensor system: sensor package 2, a laser camera and a digital camera mounted near the end of the OBSS. Sensor package 2 required an additional light source (such as daylight), had a resolution of a few millimeters and could scan at about 2.5 inches per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The crew followed "late inspection" procedures for surveying, and images of the right wing, the nose cap and much of the left wing were sent to the ground for detailed analysis.</p>
<p>Commander Kenneth Ham installed the center-line camera in the Orbiter Docking System (ODS) to help him during Atlantis' approach to the ISS. Down on the shuttle's middeck, Good and Bowen spent several hours checking out spacesuits and preparing them for transfer to the station. Reisman spent much of his day working with Antonelli and Ham on the TPS survey. He also assisted with the suit and spacewalk equipment checkouts. The crew furthermore performed the ODS ring extension that would connect the shuttle's docking port to the station's Harmony module. The last portion of the crew's day was spent preparing and checking out all of the tools to be used during the rendezvous.</p>
<p>Two course correction burns were also performed on Flight Day 2. The first 10-second burn, dubbed NC-2, was performed using the right-hand OMS engine, changing the shuttle's speed by 8 ft/s (2.4 m/s). The burn raised both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a> of the shuttle's orbit by 1-mile (1.6 km). <i>Atlantis</i>' reaction control jets were again fired for a second time to execute the 8-second NC-3 burn, which changed the shuttle's velocity by about 2 ft/s (0.61 m/s).</p>
<p>Meanwhile, Mission Control managers determined that the ISS would not need an avoidance maneuver to avoid a piece of orbital debris. Updated tracking information showed that the ISS and the debris would not pass close enough the following day to require any action.</p>

<h3><span id="16_May_(Flight_Day_3_–_Docking)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="16_May_.28Flight_Day_3_.E2.80.93_Docking.29">16 May (Flight Day 3 – Docking)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 16 May (Flight Day 3 – Docking)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The STS-132 crew began their day at 07:20 UTC and prepared to dock with the ISS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Commander Ken Ham performed a series of rendezvous burns (NH, NC4 and TI) to boost the orbit of <i>Atlantis</i> to match with that of the ISS. The longest of these, the 1-minute-24-second-long orbit raising maneuver or NH burn, changed <i>Atlantis</i>' velocity by 132 feet per second, and placed the shuttle into a new 212-by-145 miles (233 km) orbit. A 63-second circularization burn, known as NC4, boosted <i>Atlantis</i> into a 214-by-210-mile (340 km) orbit. At 11:40 UTC, with about 9 miles (14 km) separating the shuttle and the ISS, commander Ken Ham performed the final 12-second terminal initiation (TI) burn, firing the left OMS engine of <i>Atlantis</i>.</p>
<p>By 13:26 UTC, with Ken Ham flying the shuttle from the aft flight deck, <i>Atlantis</i> positioned itself beneath the ISS and began the 360-degree flip rendezvous pitch maneuver (RPM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> As the shuttle's underside rotated into view, three ISS crew members – Oleg Kotov using a camera with a 400mm lens, and Timothy Creamer and Soichi Noguchi using two 800mm-lens cameras – took 398 photographs of Atlantis' belly, as part of post-launch inspections of the thermal protection system.</p>
<p><i>Atlantis</i> docked with the ISS Pressurized Mating Adapter-2 at 14:28 UTC as the two orbited 220 miles (350 km) over the South Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> After docking, the ISS was reoriented by the small vernier thrusters on <i>Atlantis</i> to minimize the risk of Micro-Meteoroid Orbital Debris (MMOD) impacts upon the shuttle. A series of leak checks were done on both sides of the hatch by the shuttle and station crews, before the hatches were opened at 16:18 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> After a brief welcoming ceremony by the station crew, <i>Atlantis'</i> astronauts received the standard station safety briefing. The crew then got to work with initial transfers of equipment and supplies. Spacesuits were among the first items to go to the ISS. Station crew member Noguchi also transferred high-priority JAXA experiments to the Kibo module.</p>
<p>Sellers and <a href="/wiki/Expedition_23" title="Expedition 23">Expedition 23</a>/<a href="/wiki/Expedition_24" title="Expedition 24">24</a> astronaut Tracy Caldwell Dyson got to work on their joint task to relocate the ICC-VLD cargo pallet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The duo used the station's robotic arm to transfer the pallet from <i>Atlantis</i> to the station's mobile base system to prepare for the spacewalks.</p>
<p>In preparation for the following day's spacewalk, all <i>Atlantis</i>' crew members gathered for an hour-long spacewalk procedure review. Mission Specialists Reisman and Bowen spent the night in the Quest airlock as part of the overnight campout procedure to help them get prepared for the spacewalk. The crewlock was depressurized from 14.7 to 10.2 psi. The depressurization was required to avoid the formation of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> bubbles in the astronauts' blood, which could result in <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a>.</p>

<h3><span id="17_May_(Flight_Day_4_–_EVA_1)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="17_May_.28Flight_Day_4_.E2.80.93_EVA_1.29">17 May (Flight Day 4 – EVA 1)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 17 May (Flight Day 4 – EVA 1)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2828" data-file-width="4288" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg/220px-Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg/330px-Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg/440px-Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reisman_Self_Portrait_STS-132_EVA_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reisman takes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self_portrait" title="Self portrait">self portrait</a> during EVA 1.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the morning wakeup call, Mission Control CAPCOM <a href="/wiki/Shannon_Lucid" title="Shannon Lucid">Shannon Lucid</a> informed the shuttle crew that no detailed flight inspection would be required on the next day. However, the crew were requested to utilize that time to do inspections on various sections of <i>Atlantis</i> that were not inspected on flight day 2.</p>
<p>Flight day 4 saw Mission Specialists Garret Reisman and Steve Bowen perform the first of three planned spacewalks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The pair installed a spare Space To Ground Antenna (SGANT), a new enhanced tool platform for the Special Purpose Dexterous Manipulator (SPDM, also known as <a href="/wiki/Dextre" title="Dextre">Dextre</a>) and released torque on the six new batteries for the Port 6 (P6) truss segment.</p>
<p>Expedition 23 Flight Engineer Creamer helped the duo with their suit-up preparations. Mike Good joined STS-132 Pilot Antonelli, the intravehicular officer, to assist during the spacewalk. Mission Specialist Sellers and station Flight Engineer Caldwell Dyson operated the robotic arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Throughout EVA 1, Commander Ken Ham oversaw the extravehicular activities.</p>
<p>During the spacewalk, several problems were encountered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> the first of which was during installation of the SGANT. A slight gap was observed between the antenna dish and its mounting pole. The spacewalkers loosened the bolts and used a higher torque setting, which managed to close the gap to a smaller width. The launch locks were left on the SGANT to allow engineers on the ground to determine if the gap was acceptable, or if more troubleshooting would be needed. The second problem occurred during the installation of the SGANT, and was related to the Command and Control (CNC) computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> During installation, when Steve Bowen removed a cover from a connector, the prime CNC computer detected an error and shut down. The cap was a special cap which allowed the circuit for that connector to be closed, so when it was opened the sensor detected an error. The shut down of the CNC caused a 2-minute loss of communications. The safeing of the computer also stopped the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadarm2" title="Canadarm2">Canadarm2</a> for a reconfiguration of the cameras being used during the spacewalk by both the robotic arm operators and observers on the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat7_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-stat7-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The spacewalk ended at 19:19 UTC, after Reisman and Bowen made an inventory of the tools they brought with them and made their way back into the Quest airlock. STS-132's EVA 1 was the 237th conducted by U.S. astronauts, the second for Reisman and the fourth for Bowen. It was also the 144th in support of ISS assembly and maintenance. For EVA 1, lead spacewalker Reisman had a spacesuit with no stripes. Bowen' spacesuit was marked with a red stripe.</p>
<p>The shuttle's robotic arm also successfully grappled the MRM-1 module in <i>Atlantis</i>' cargo bay in preparation for its berthing to the Zarya service module the following day.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">

<h3><span id="18_May_(Flight_Day_5_–_MRM1_installation)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="18_May_.28Flight_Day_5_.E2.80.93_MRM1_installation.29">18 May (Flight Day 5 – MRM1 installation)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 18 May (Flight Day 5 – MRM1 installation)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG/220px-SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG/330px-SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG/440px-SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpaceStation_STS132_Tampa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Atlantis</i> docked with the ISS moving southeast across the skies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa" title="Tampa">Tampa</a>, Florida.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On flight day 5, the crew focused on the addition of the MRM-1 module to the space station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Commander Ken Ham and pilot Tony Antonelli maneuvered <i>Atlantis</i>' robotic arm to unberth MRM-1 from the shuttle's payload bay at 09:49 UTC, and handed it off to the station's Canadarm2 at 10:14 UTC. Mission Specialists Garrett Reisman and Piers Sellers, working from inside the station's Cupola, then maneuvered the Canadarm2 arm to deliver MRM-1 to its new position, the Earth-facing port of the Zarya service module. The docking occurred at 12:20 UTC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> when the shuttle-station stack was flying above Argentina. Following the successful docking, Sellers reported to Mission Control that, during the docking, he did not see the expected "capture 1" confirmation signal appear on his laptop, to which CAPCOM Steve Swanson replied "And station, that error's expected. The reason you didn't get 'contact 1' is because Garrett did too good of a job flying. He went right down the middle and got a hole in one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Expedition 23 Commander Oleg Kotov also monitored the activities from the Russian segment, as the MRM-1 began its automated docking sequence for the final attachment to the Zarya module. The berthing marked the first time that the Russian automated docking system has been used along with the station's robotic arm.</p>
<p>At 17:20 UTC, shuttle crew members Ham, Reisman, Sellers, along with station crew members Kotov, Skvortsov and Caldwell Dyson, gathered in the space station's Harmony module to talk with reporters from <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a> and <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The two crews answered questions related to their stay in orbit, medical experiments being conducted on the ISS, spacewalking experiences and the <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Gulf of Mexico oil spill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>After midday, Reisman and Sellers used Canadarm2 to unberth the OBSS from the sill of <i>Atlantis</i>' cargo bay and handed it off to the shuttle's robotic arm, operated by Ham and Antonelli.</p>
<p>Mission Specialists Bowen and Good prepared for next day's EVA 2, configuring their tools and preparing their spacesuits. At the end of the workday, Atlantis' crew, along with three station crew members, met for an hour-long spacewalk procedure review.</p>
<p>At 21:45 UTC, Good and Bowen began their campout inside the Quest airlock, with pressure reduced to 10.2 psi to reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a>.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">

</div>

<h3><span id="19_May_(Flight_Day_6_–_EVA_2)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="19_May_.28Flight_Day_6_.E2.80.93_EVA_2.29">19 May (Flight Day 6 – EVA 2)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 19 May (Flight Day 6 – EVA 2)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2828" data-file-width="4288" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg/220px-STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg/330px-STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg/440px-STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS132_EVA2_Mike_Good1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mike Good during EVA 2.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The primary task on the mission's second spacewalk, EVA 2, was to remove and replace batteries on the station's port 6 truss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The spacewalk got under way at 10:38 UTC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> more than 25 minutes ahead of the scheduled start, which already had been moved up 30 minutes.</p>
<p>Lead spacewalker Bowen's first task was to remove a cable snag in the OBSS's pan and tilt mechanism. Bowen looped a tie wrap on two cables to relieve the snag and completed the task in less than 30 minutes, while Good began work with the batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Although the initial plan was to replace three batteries, the two astronauts managed to replace an additional fourth battery during EVA 2. The batteries Bowen and Good replaced had originally been launched in November 2000. After the battery work and cleanup of the area, Bowen and Good moved on to the new backup K<sub>u</sub> band antenna on the Z1 truss. They tightened bolts holding the antenna's dish to its boom, closing a gap left there after EVA 1. Good performed a wiggle test and confirmed that two spacewalkers did not see any signs of motion in the antenna-mast interface. They then removed the antenna's launch locks, leaving the antenna ready to operate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>During EVA 2, commander Ken Ham provided photo and television support, and pilot Tony Antonelli served as the spacewalk choreographer. ISS crew member Tracy Caldwell Dyson also assisted with spacewalk preparations. EVA 2 marked the 238th conducted by U.S. astronauts, the fifth for Bowen and the third for Good. It was also the 145th in support of International Space Station assembly and maintenance.</p>
<h3><span id="20_May_(Flight_Day_7_–_MRM-1_initial_checks,_transfers_and_off-duty)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="20_May_.28Flight_Day_7_.E2.80.93_MRM-1_initial_checks.2C_transfers_and_off-duty.29">20 May (Flight Day 7 – MRM-1 initial checks, transfers and off-duty)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 20 May (Flight Day 7 – MRM-1 initial checks, transfers and off-duty)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2834" data-file-width="4288" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg/220px-STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg/330px-STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg/440px-STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS132_Good_Bowen_Reisman_fd7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Good, Bowen and Reisman pose for a photo with an Extravehicular Mobility Unit (EMU).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On flight day 7, the crew spent a few hours of off-duty time in the afternoon, but otherwise were largely focused on preparations for EVA 3. Earlier in the day, at 10:52 UTC, following leak checks, ISS Expedition 23 commander Oleg Kotov and flight engineer Alexander Skvortsov opened the hatch to the MRM-1 module.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> They wore eye and breathing protection as a standard precaution when entering a new module. Kotov reported that some metal filings were drifting around inside the new module as unpacking activities gathered pace, although initially he reported the interior of MRM-1 looked clean. Flight controllers both in Houston and Moscow worked with the crew to develop a technique for safely removing the floating debris.</p>
<p>At 12:25 UTC, shuttle crew members Ken Ham, Tony Antonelli, Piers Sellers, and ISS flight engineer Tracy Caldwell Dyson, talked with the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox_News_Radio" title="Fox News Radio">Fox News Radio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Ham also joined in with past and present members of Mission Control to recognize Lonnie J. Schmitt as the first flight controller to reach his 100th shuttle mission.</p>
<p>During the day, Ham, Antonelli and Sellers transferred equipment, supplies and experiments between <i>Atlantis</i> and the ISS. Mission Specialists Mike Good and Garrett Reisman prepared for EVA 3, configuring tools and preparing suits and the Quest airlock. Ham, Antonelli and Sellers also joined them to review the procedures. As part of the campout procedure, the two spacewalkers spent the night in the Quest airlock, with its air pressure reduced to 10.2 psi.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

<h3><span id="21_May_(Flight_Day_8_–_EVA_3)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="21_May_.28Flight_Day_8_.E2.80.93_EVA_3.29">21 May (Flight Day 8 – EVA 3)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 21 May (Flight Day 8 – EVA 3)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On flight day 8, Mike Good and Garret Reisman completed EVA 3, the third and final spacewalk of the STS-132 mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The pair connected a pair of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> jumpers on the P4/P5 truss segment, before continuing on out to the end of the P6 truss. Once at the P6 truss, Good and Reisman completed the battery swap by removing and replacing the final two batteries and retrieving the temporarily stowed old battery on the truss. Once that task was complete, Good and Reisman moved to <i>Atlantis</i>'s payload bay, where they removed a grapple fixture and took it to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quest_airlock" title="Quest airlock">Quest airlock</a>. The pair then moved on to fix some insulation on the <a href="/wiki/Dextre" title="Dextre">Dextre</a> robot, and stowed some tools in an external toolbox on the Z1 truss. Pilot Tony Antonelli choreographed the spacewalk from inside the shuttle.</p>
<p>While the spacewalk was going on, Commander Ken Ham and Mission Specialist Steve Bowen completed some more of the transfer work for the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat15_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-stat15-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">

<h3><span id="22_May_(Flight_day_9_–_ICC-VLD_re-installation_and_off-duty)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="22_May_.28Flight_day_9_.E2.80.93_ICC-VLD_re-installation_and_off-duty.29">22 May (Flight day 9 – ICC-VLD re-installation and off-duty)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 22 May (Flight day 9 – ICC-VLD re-installation and off-duty)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2116" data-file-width="3319" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/220px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/330px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/440px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The newly upgraded ISS, as seen from the shuttle after undocking.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Flight day 9 saw the shuttle crew enjoying some off-duty time during the afternoon. In the morning, the entire crew participated in some transfer activities and orbiter maintenance. The ICC-VLD was also berthed back aboard <i>Atlantis</i>' payload bay, having completed its tasks for this mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadarm2" title="Canadarm2">Canadarm2</a> was used to return the ICC-VLD to the bay, and was operated by Mission Specialists Piers Sellers and Garrett Reisman and space station flight engineer <a href="/wiki/Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson" title="Tracy Caldwell Dyson">Tracy Caldwell Dyson</a>. The ICC-VLD re-installation operation began just after 4:30 am EDT, and was completed at 5:50 am EDT. The shuttle crew and Caldwell Dyson also answered some questions from elementary- and middle-school students from around the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat17_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-stat17-86">[86]</a></sup> Students from 12 NASA Explorer Schools had submitted their questions earlier by video. The combined shuttle-station crew also shared a joint meal before the shuttle crew enjoyed two and a half hours of off-duty time starting at 11:05 am EDT.</p>
<h3><span id="23_May_(Flight_day_10_–_Undocking)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="23_May_.28Flight_day_10_.E2.80.93_Undocking.29">23 May (Flight day 10 – Undocking)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 23 May (Flight day 10 – Undocking)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On flight day 10, the joint STS-132/<a href="/wiki/Expedition_23" title="Expedition 23">Expedition 23</a> crews awoke to begin the final hours of the joint docked mission. The crews completed the final time-sensitive transfers of the mission, which included scientific research samples that need to be kept cold. Once these transfers were completed, the two crews held a joint crew news conference and took a crew photo, and later, prior to hatch closure, held a mutual farewell ceremony. After the ceremony, the hatches between <i>Atlantis</i> and the International Space Station were closed, and a leak check was performed to ensure all the hatches were sealed properly. The shuttle undocked from the ISS at 15:22 UTC, a little more than 2 hours after the hatches were closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> At the time of the undocking, the two spacecraft were orbiting 220 miles (350 km) above the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> southwest of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perth,_Western_Australia" title="Perth, Western Australia">Perth, Australia</a>. The shuttle, guided by pilot <a href="/wiki/Dominic_A._Antonelli" title="Dominic A. Antonelli">Tony Antonelli</a>, backed away from the ISS to a distance of about 400 feet (120 m), at which time Antonelli began conducting a fly-around of the space station, so that crew members on both the ISS and shuttle could get photos of both vehicles. Once the fly-around was complete, the shuttle crew conducted two separation burns to move <i>Atlantis</i> away from the space station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat19_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-stat19-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">

<h3><span id="24_May_(Flight_day_11_–_Late_inspection)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="24_May_.28Flight_day_11_.E2.80.93_Late_inspection.29">24 May (Flight day 11 – Late inspection)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 24 May (Flight day 11 – Late inspection)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The crew of <i>Atlantis</i> awoke on flight day 11, and after a couple of hours of personal time, began the late inspection of the shuttle's wing leading edges and nose cap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The crew finished the scans about two and a half hours ahead of schedule. By 09:50 UTC, they had finished their look at the right wing, by 10:52 UTC the nose cap survey was complete, and the left wing survey was finished at 11:17 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The TPS survey was done using the shuttle's robotic arm and its OBSS extension. While the scans were going on, some of the crew stowed items that were no longer needed or were transferred right before undocking. Spacewalkers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mike_Good" title="Mike Good">Mike Good</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steve_Bowen" title="Steve Bowen">Steve Bowen</a> cleaned up and stowed their spacesuits for landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The latter part of the crew's day was spent with some off-duty time.</p>
<h3><span id="25_May_(Flight_day_12_–_Landing_prep)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="25_May_.28Flight_day_12_.E2.80.93_Landing_prep.29">25 May (Flight day 12 – Landing prep)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 25 May (Flight day 12 – Landing prep)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Atlantis</i>' astronauts devoted flight day 12 to preparing for the shuttle's landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The crew executed standard day-before-landing activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Commander Ham, Pilot Antonelli and Mission Specialist Good began the flight control system (FCS) hot-fire checkout at about 1:40 am EDT, operating the rudder and flaps that would control <i>Atlantis</i>' flight through the atmosphere to the KSC runway. That complete, Ham and Antonelli fired each of the shuttle's 44 attitude control thrusters, which were designed to orient <i>Atlantis</i> in space as it descended from orbit and through the upper atmosphere. Both these tests were completed successfully.</p>
<p>All STS-132 crew members worked at various times throughout the day to stow items in the cabin to prepare for landing. They also gathered for a 30-minute deorbit briefing at 5:40 am EDT. Immediately afterward, the crew talked with representatives of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colbert_Report" title="Colbert Report">Colbert Report</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_Radio_Network" title="ABC Radio Network">ABC Radio Network</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WEWS-TV" title="WEWS-TV">WEWS-TV</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland, Ohio</a>.</p>
<p>Late in the day, Mission Specialists Reisman and Sellers stowed the K<sub>u</sub> band antenna in <i>Atlantis</i>' cargo bay.</p>
<h3><span id="26_May_(Flight_day_13_–_Re-entry_and_landing)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="26_May_.28Flight_day_13_.E2.80.93_Re-entry_and_landing.29">26 May (Flight day 13 – Re-entry and landing)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=STS-132&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 26 May (Flight day 13 – Re-entry and landing)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-132_landing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1996" data-file-width="3000" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/STS-132_landing.jpg/220px-STS-132_landing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/STS-132_landing.jpg/330px-STS-132_landing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/STS-132_landing.jpg/440px-STS-132_landing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-132_landing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
STS-132 ends as Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> lands on 26 May 2010, at Kennedy Space Center's <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Landing_Facility" title="Shuttle Landing Facility">Shuttle Landing Facility</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_8" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:480px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_9" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/STS-132_landing.ogv/480px--STS-132_landing.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:480px;height:270px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="690.75742409076" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="STS-132_landing.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/STS-132_landing.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 480 × 270 (477 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="480" data-height="270" data-bandwidth="477210" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/55/STS-132_landing.ogv/STS-132_landing.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="204408" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/55/STS-132_landing.ogv/STS-132_landing.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="317344" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:STS-132 landing.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/STS-132_landing.ogv/220px--STS-132_landing.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/STS-132_landing.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-132_landing.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>Landing video</b> (11 mins 31 secs)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The STS-132 crew awoke at 12:20 EDT (4:20 UTC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> At about 7:40 UTC, the astronauts began deorbit preparations, and closed the payload bay doors at 9:01 UTC. The deorbit burn initiated at 11:42 UTC, 220 miles (350 km) above <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, and terminated at 11:45. At an altitude of 400,000 feet (120,000 m) and a speed of <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a> 25, <i>Atlantis</i> began re-entry at 12:16. At about 12:23 UTC, <i>Atlantis</i> began its s-rolls, to bleed off speed and energy during re-entry. At about 12:29, the shuttle was more than 40 miles (64 km) above the Earth and 2,000 miles (3,200 km) from KSC, traveling at Mach 22. At 12:34, <i>Atlantis</i> was about 180,000 feet (55,000 m) up, traveling at about 9,200 mph, and was 600 miles (970 km) from the runway. At about the same time, the shuttle was experiencing maximum re-entry heating conditions, peaking at about 2,900 degrees <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">Fahrenheit</a>, lasting about two minutes. At 12:39, long-range cameras at KSC spotted the shuttle gliding towards the runway at an altitude of 16 miles (26 km) and a distance of 77 miles (124 km) from KSC. At 12:44, commander Ken Ham took manual control of the orbiter for landing as it glided below the 50,000-foot (15,000 m) mark.</p>
<p><i>Atlantis</i> landed on its main wheels on runway 33 of Kennedy Space Center's <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Landing_Facility" title="Shuttle Landing Facility">Shuttle Landing Facility</a> at 08:48:11 EDT (12:48:11 UTC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The nose wheel touched down 10 seconds later, at 08:48:21 EDT (12:48:11 UTC), with the vehicle coming to a stop at 08:49:18 EDT (12:49:18 UTC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The entire mission lasted 11 days, 18 hours, 29 minutes, and 9 seconds, during which time the space shuttle traveled a total of 7,724,851 kilometers (4,800,000 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The six astronauts headed to Houston on 27 May. A welcome ceremony for the crew was held at 5 pm EDT that same day at Ellington Field's NASA Hangar 276.</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
